She opens her eyes.

The pianoforte keys.

Her hands.

The morning light.

The flame —

Is still there.

That is the thing.

The kiss did not resolve it.

Did not close the chapter.

Did not —

As she had perhaps —

Privately —

Hoped —

Make it manageable.

It is —

Still.

There.

Burning with the particular quality of things that have been named.

Which is —

More.

Than things unnamed.

Considerably.

She plays.

Again.

The complicated piece.

The one that arrived this morning.

Both melodies.

Simultaneously.

The one that resolves.

And the one that is — still — asking.

---

### XVII. Watch Wait Protect

VIOLET — THE MORNING ROOM

After.

After the family dispersed.

After Eloise went back to the library.

After Gregory went on his errand.

After Hyacinth went wherever Hyacinth goes when she has information she is deciding what to do with.

After Colin returned to Penelope.

After all of it —

Violet.

Alone.

In the morning room.

Her writing desk.

The correspondence.

The pen she did not pick back up.

She sits.

With the particular quality of a woman who is holding several things.

Simultaneously.

With the care they each deserve.

Eloise.

The letter.

*He said yes.*

Her daughter's face.

Open.

Completely.

The window unlocked.

The air —

Getting in.

After a very long time.

The relief of it.

The warmth of it.

Real.

Complete.

And.

The hallway.

Your face.

Coming out of the music room.

The specific quality of a man who has just done something difficult and correct and is — despite it — carrying something he did not expect to be carrying.

Something he came into that room without.

Something —

She knows.

What it was.

She was a mother before she was anything else in this house.

She knows Francesca's music.

The way it changes when something has shifted.

She stood in that hallway.

Long enough.

She heard.

The complicated piece.

That had not existed —

Before this morning.

She picks up the pen.

Puts it down.

Picks it up.

Puts it down.

Looks at the flowers.

The same ones she arranged yesterday.

Still correct.

Still where they belong.

She is not —

She tells herself carefully —

Angry.

She is not angry.

Neither of them —

Did anything wrong.

He came here to be honest.

He was honest.

Francesca —

Francesca did what Francesca does.

What Francesca has always done.

In every room she has ever stood in.

The exact honest thing.

Without performance.

Without strategy.

Because she cannot do otherwise.

Because Francesca Bridgerton —

Constitutionally —

Cannot be false.

And Violet —

Who has been measuring love against the impossible standard of Edmund —

Understands.

What it is to feel something —

Completely —

And have it be —

The wrong time.

The wrong direction.

The wrong everything.

Except the feeling itself.

Which is —

Undeniably —

Real.

She thinks about Francesca.

In the music room.

The piece she is — currently — playing.

That Violet can hear faintly.

Through the walls.

The complicated one.

The new one.

Both melodies.

Simultaneously.

She thinks about Eloise.

In the library.

Actually reading.

For the first time all week.

The particular quality of a woman whose mind has been set free from its own management.

Temporarily.

Beautifully.

She thinks about you.

Giordhyn Zaff.

Who came to her first.

Before the Season.

Before the folio.

Before anything.

Who said — *they are worth more than what I want.*

Who told her — *home* — about Francesca.

And — *not alone* — about Eloise.

Who came this morning and was honest.

At considerable personal cost.

And came out of the music room —

Carrying something.

That she will have to address.

When the time is right.

When Eloise is further in.

When Francesca has found her footing.

When the Season has had time to —

Settle.

Around the shape of what is true.

She picks up the pen.

This time —

Writes.

Not correspondence.

Not the Friday letters.

Simply —

A note.

To herself.

In the private shorthand she developed across twenty-seven years of managing things that required managing.

Three words.

*Watch. Wait. Protect.*

She folds it.

Into her pocket.

Private.

Hers.

For the moment when she will need to remember what she decided this morning.

In this room.

Alone.

With the flowers.

And the pen.

And the faint sound of a complicated melody.

Through the walls.

She picks up the real correspondence.

Begins.

The Friday letters.

The ordinary.

Which is not —

Today —

Ordinary.

But which she will treat as though it is.

Because that is what you do.

When you are the center.

And the family turns around you.

And you hold the things they cannot yet hold.

Until they can.

The morning room.

The flowers.

The light.

The pen moving.

Steadily.

The way it always moves.

Regardless.

And through the walls —

Faintly —

The music.

Both melodies.

Simultaneously.

The one that resolves.

And the one —

Still —

Asking.

*End of Chapter Thirty-Five.*

---

## Chapter Thirty-Six: The Afternoon Reckoning

---

### I. The Weight Increases

The morning passes.

The Friday letters.

The correspondence.

The ordinary machinery of the household.

Moving.

As it always moves.

Around Violet.

Who moves it.

But underneath —

The thing she is carrying.

Quietly.

With the care it deserves.

And the weight it has.

She finishes the letters.

Gives them to the footman.

Stands.

Goes to the window.

The morning room window.

The garden.

The roses.

Not yet.

---

### II. Listening

She listens.

The house around her.

Gregory — somewhere upstairs.

Returned from his errand.

Hyacinth — the sound of her moving through the first floor.

The particular Hyacinth momentum.

Colin and Penelope — their study.

Quiet.

Which means they are talking.

The real kind.

Eloise — the library.

Silence.

Which means she is actually reading.

Finally.

And Francesca.

The music room.

The complicated piece.

Still.

Playing.

Into the afternoon.

Violet stands at the window.

For a long time.

Not moving.

Simply —

Listening.

To the piece.

The two melodies.

Simultaneously.

Learning their shape.

From the outside.

The way Benedetti listened from the corridor.

For the intention.

Not the performance.

She knows.

What the intention is.

She has been a mother for twenty-seven years.

She knows Francesca's music.

Every piece.

Every version.

The grief pieces.

The comfort pieces.

The pieces that mean — *I am fine* — which Francesca plays when she is not fine.

The pieces that mean — *I am finding my way* — which she has been playing since the musicale.

This piece —

Is new.

She has never heard it before.

It arrived this morning.

After you left.

And it has not stopped.

It means —

Something Violet hoped she would not have to hear.

Something she suspected.

From the hallway.

From your face.

From the specific quality of a man coming out of a room he did not go into the same.

She turns from the window.

---

### III. The Music Room Door

THE MUSIC ROOM — FRIDAY AFTERNOON

She does not knock.

She never knocks on the music room door.

This is the one room in Bridgerton House where Violet does not knock.

Because knocking on the music room door —

In her experience of twenty-seven years —

Makes Francesca stop.

And Francesca stopping is always a loss.

She opens the door.

Quietly.

Enters.

Closes it behind her.

Francesca —

At the pianoforte.

Does not stop.

Which means —

She was expecting this.

Or —

She is too far inside the music to stop.

Or both.

Probably both.

Violet sits.

The chair near the window.

The one you sat in this morning.

She does not announce herself.

She simply —

Sits.

And listens.

---

### IV. Five Minutes

The piece.

Up close.

More complicated than it sounded through the walls.

The two melodies —

More distinct.

More —

Deliberately distinct.

Not accidental countermelody.

Chosen.

Two things.

Held.

Simultaneously.

With the full technical skill of a woman who has been playing since childhood.

Applied — for the first time —

To something entirely personal.

Violet listens.

For a long time.

Five minutes.

Perhaps more.

Neither of them speaking.

The room holding them.

The music holding the room.

Then —

The piece resolves.

Or —

Doesn't quite.

It arrives at something.

Not the clean resolution of the musicale.

Something more —

Open ended.

As though the composer —

Francesca —

Could not find the ending.

Because the ending has not yet arrived.

The last note.

Held.

Then —

Released.

Fading.

Into the afternoon.

Francesca's hands —

In her lap.

She does not turn.

---

### V. The Hallway

"Mama," she says.

"Yes," Violet says.

A silence.

The room.

The afternoon light.

"You were in the hallway," Francesca says.

Not a question.

"Yes," Violet says.

"When he left."

"Yes."

"You saw his face."

"Yes."

Francesca turns.

On the bench.

Slowly.

To face her mother.

Her expression —

The composure.

Present.

But —

Thinner than usual.

The way ice thins.

In late winter.

Without breaking.

But —

Clearly.

Changed.

---

### VI. The Gap

They look at each other.

Mother and daughter.

The most complex relationship in this family.

The one that has always lived in the gap between two languages.

Violet's — warm, direct, orchestrating.

Francesca's — quiet, internal, musical.

Both real.

Both love.

Both —

Struggling always —

To cross the gap.

Today —

The gap —

Smaller.

Perhaps because Francesca is too tired to maintain it.

Perhaps because what happened this morning is too large to be held in the usual architecture.

Perhaps because —

Simply —

The time for it has passed.

"Tell me," Violet says.

The same words she said to Eloise.

This morning.

In this same house.

The same request.

From the same mother.

To a completely different daughter.

In a completely different language.

---

### VII. He Was Honest

Francesca looks at her hands.

"He told me about Eloise," she says.

"Yes."

"He came here — before the letter arrived — to tell me himself."

"Yes."

"That he had chosen her."

"Yes."

A pause.

"He was honest," Francesca says.

"He was — entirely — honest."

"Yes."

"He told me my recovery was my own."

"Yes."

"That I would have found the music again without him."

"Yes."

A pause.

"Was he right?" Violet asks.

Carefully.

Francesca considers this.

For a long time.

The honest consideration.

Not the managed version.

"Yes," she says finally.

"I think so."

"I was — already — coming back."

"Before he arrived."

"He was simply—"

"Present," Violet says.

"At the right moment."

"Yes."

---

### VIII. The Kiss Told

A pause.

"But—"

Violet waits.

The Francesca pace.

Learned across twenty-three years.

Of waiting.

For the internal processing to become external.

Of not pushing.

Of simply —

Being present.

Until the words arrive.

In their own time.

"Mama."

"Yes."

"I kissed him."

The music room.

The afternoon.

The last note —

Still fading.

Somewhere in the air.

Violet is —

Still.

The way she is still under pressure.

The composure.

The iron.

Both.

Fully deployed.

"Yes," she says.

Quietly.

"I thought — something—"

"I know," Francesca says.

"You were in the hallway."

"Yes."

"I heard the door."

"I'm sorry—"

"Don't," Francesca says.

"Don't apologize for knowing."

"You always know."

"I don't—"

"You always know, Mama," she says.

"It has always — frightened me slightly."

A pause.

The faintest thing — not quite warmth — between them.

The specific warmth of honesty replacing management.

"I know," Violet says.

"I'm sorry for that."

"No—"

"I have — pushed, sometimes."

"Mama."

"More than I should."

"Yes," Francesca says.

"But—"

She looks at her hands.

"But you are here now," she says.

"And not pushing."

"And that is—"

A pause.

"That is enough."

---

### IX. The Flame Named

The room.

The afternoon.

Both of them —

Inside something real.

For possibly the first time.

In a very long time.

Perhaps ever.

"Tell me about the kiss," Violet says.

Gently.

No judgment in it.

Simply —

The request.

To be let in.

All the way.

For once.

Francesca looks at the pianoforte.

At the keys.

"I knew," she says.

"When he told me about Eloise."

"That it was right."

"I knew — immediately."

"The rightness of it."

"Which was—"

"Harder than if it had been wrong," Violet says.

"Yes."

"Because wrong would have given you something to argue with."

"Yes."

"And this—"

"Had nothing to argue with," Francesca says.

"Yes."

A pause.

"And then?"

Francesca turns.

Fully.

Faces her mother.

The full look.

The depth.

The ocean.

"Mama," she says.

"I have to tell you something."

"About John."

"And about—"

She stops.

"About what I understood this morning."

"That I did not know before."

---

### X. What Was Missing

Violet —

Very still.

"Tell me," she says.

Francesca looks at the pianoforte.

At the keys.

At her hands.

Then —

At her mother.

"I loved John," she says.

"Yes."

"Completely."

"Yes."

"He was — the right instrument."

"Yes."

"And our marriage was—"

She stops.

Looks for the word.

"Complete," she says.

"In its way."

"Yes."

"But—"

A long pause.

"Mama."

"Yes."

"There was never — any—"

She cannot say it.

She does not have —

As she told you this morning —

The vocabulary.

Not because she is innocent.

Because she has never needed the words.

Because the thing has never —

Until this morning —

Required naming.

Violet —

Understands.

Before the sentence is complete.

The way mothers understand things.

Before they are said.

Because they have been watching.

For twenty-three years.

And some things —

When you have been watching carefully enough —

Speak for themselves.

"I know," Violet says.

Very quietly.

Francesca looks at her.

Surprised.

"You—"

"I know," Violet says again.

"I have always — suspected."

"Not because of John — he was — he loved you correctly."

"But—"

She pauses.

"You are — constitutionally — someone who feels deeply," she says.

"And John was — constitutionally — someone who was — gentle."

"And gentle is — real."

"Gentle is — right."

"But—"

"But gentle is not — all of it," Francesca says.

"No," Violet says.

"It is not."

---

### XI. Edmund

A pause.

"Edmund was not — only — gentle," Violet says.

"No."

"No," Francesca says.

"I know."

A beat.

"I always knew," she says.

"That you had something I—"

She stops.

"That I did not."

"With John."

"And I thought—"

"That perhaps it was not for everyone," Violet says.

"Yes."

"That perhaps it was—"

"Enough," Violet says.

"Yes," Francesca says.

"That what John and I had was — sufficient."

"And it was," Violet says.

"Yes."

"It was — real and right and — sufficient."

"But—"

"But this morning," Francesca says.

"Yes."

"I understood."

"What I had been missing."

"Not from John."

"Not from the marriage."

"Simply—"

"From yourself," Violet says.

"Yes," Francesca says.

"From — the full version of what is — possible."

The music room.

The afternoon.

Both of them sitting with this.

The specific weight of a truth that has been present for a long time.

And has only now been said aloud.

In the right room.

With the right person.

---

### XII. Can You Let Him Go

Violet —

Is not —

She tells herself again —

Angry.

She is —

Frightened.

The specific fear of a mother who has two daughters.

Both of whom are —

Currently —

Holding something that involves the same man.

And who do not —

Yet —

Know that the other is holding it.

"Francesca," she says.

"Yes."

"I need to ask you something."

"Yes."

"And I need you to answer me honestly."

"I always answer honestly."

"Yes," Violet says.

"You do."

"It is one of the things about you that I have—"

She pauses.

"Loved most."

"And found most — difficult."

"Simultaneously."

Francesca almost smiles.

"Yes," she says.

"I know."

"He has chosen Eloise," Violet says.

"Yes."

"And that choice is—"

"Right," Francesca says.

"Yes."

"I know it is right."

"And yet—"

Violet pauses.

"The kiss."

"Yes."

"Was yours."

"Yes."

"You chose it."

"Yes."

"Knowing—"

"Everything," Francesca says.

"Yes."

"Knowing he had chosen her."

"Knowing it was — already decided."

"Knowing that it could not — change anything."

"Yes."

A pause.

"And it didn't," Francesca says.

"Change anything."

"He still chose her."

"Yes."

"He is right to choose her."

"Yes."

Violet looks at her daughter.

"Then—"

She stops.

Starts again.

More carefully.

"Francesca."

"Yes."

"Can you—"

She searches for the right words.

The right language.

Francesca's language.

Not the Violet version.

The Francesca version.

"Can you let him go?" she asks.

"To Eloise."

"Without — carrying this — in a way that—"

"Hurts everyone," Francesca says.

"Yes."

---

### XIII. No

A long silence.

The longest of the afternoon.

Francesca —

Looking at the pianoforte.

The keys.

The sheet music.

The complicated piece she played this morning.

That has no ending yet.

When she speaks —

Her voice is —

Even.

Still.

Francesca.

Completely.

"No," she says.

The music room.

Very still.

"Francesca—"

"No," she says again.

Not louder.

Not defiant.

Simply —

Honest.

The way she is always —

Constitutionally —

Honest.

Even when honesty is —

The most complicated possible thing.

---

### XIV. Cannot Be False

"I cannot tell you," she says, "that I can simply — let it go."

"Because I cannot."

"I have tried — since this morning — to locate the part of myself that would — simply — accept it and move on."

"And I cannot find it."

"Not because I am — selfish—"

"No," Violet says.

"Not because I want to hurt Eloise—"

"I know."

"Not because I don't understand — clearly — that he has chosen correctly."

"I know."

A pause.

"But—"

She looks at her mother.

The full look.

"Mama."

"Yes."

"For twenty years," she says, "I have been — very good — at not wanting things."

"Yes."

"At finding — the exact right amount of wanting — that could be satisfied by what was available."

"Yes."

"John was — that."

"He was — exactly — what was available."

"And I loved him for it."

"And I am glad of every moment."

"Yes."

---

### XV. Cannot Unknow It

"But—"

She stops.

Her hands —

Finding the keys.

Not playing.

Simply —

Touching.

The way she touches things that help her think.

"This morning," she says.

"Something arrived."

"That I did not know was — possible — for me."

"Specifically."

"And I—"

A breath.

"I cannot unknow it," she says.

"I cannot — pretend it didn't arrive."

"I cannot — perform acceptance — of a thing I do not — actually — accept."

"Because I cannot be false," she says.

"You know this."

"Yes."

"I am constitutionally — incapable of it."

"Yes."

"So when you ask me if I can let him go—"

"I can tell you that I will not — interfere."

"I can tell you that I understand the rightness of his choice."

"I can tell you that I love Eloise — completely — as my sister."

"And that I would not — for anything — deliberately cause her pain."

"All of that is true."

"Yes," Violet says.

"Yes."

---

### XVI. Not Even for Eloise

"But—"

Francesca looks at her mother.

"I cannot tell you," she says,

"that I have stopped wanting."

"What arrived this morning."

"Because that would be — false."

"And I cannot be false."

"Not even—"

"For Eloise," Violet says.

Quietly.

"Not even," Francesca says,

"for Eloise."

---

### XVII. What Do You Need

The music room.

The afternoon.

The light —

Moving.

The way light moves.

Regardless.

Violet —

Looks at her daughter.

The full look.

The mother look.

That sees everything.

And has to —

In this moment —

Hold everything it sees.

Without the luxury of resolution.

Without the architecture of arrangement.

Simply —

Hold it.

The complexity of it.

In full.

Eloise — in the library.

Actually reading.

The window unlocked.

The air — getting in.

After a very long time.

Happy.

The private, complete kind.

Francesca — at the pianoforte.

Hands on the keys.

The complicated piece.

Both melodies.

Simultaneously.

The one that resolves.

And the one —

Still —

Asking.

Refusing —

Honestly —

To stop wanting.

What arrived.

This morning.

Before she knew what it was.

And you —

Across the park.

In the Zaff townhouse.

Carrying two true things.

With the pocket watch.

And the weight of a morning that changed something.

In the specific way that mornings can change things.

Without asking permission.

Without announcing themselves.

Simply —

Arriving.

And staying.

Violet —

Takes a long breath.

Slow.

The iron holding.

The warmth —

Still there.

Underneath.

Both.

"What do you need?" she asks.

Finally.

The only question that matters.

From a mother.

To a daughter.

Who has just been completely, devastatingly honest.

In the language she actually speaks.

Without translation.

Without management.

For the first time.

---

### XVIII. Stay

Francesca looks at the keys.

For a long moment.

"I need," she says,

"to play."

A pause.

"And I need you to stay."

"While I do."

"And not—"

"Say anything," Violet says.

"Yes."

"Simply — stay."

"Yes."

"Can you do that?"

Violet —

Who has spent twenty-three years talking.

Managing.

Orchestrating.

Loving loudly.

In the language her daughter cannot always receive.

Who has been — practicing —

The other thing.

The quieter thing.

The staying without requiring anything of the staying.

"Yes," she says.

"I can do that."

Francesca turns.

To the pianoforte.

Hands on the keys.

The complicated piece.

Beginning again.

From the start.

Both melodies.

Simultaneously.

Violet —

In the chair.

The one you sat in.

This morning.

Her hands —

In her lap.

Her pen —

Upstairs.

The correspondence —

Done.

The note —

In her pocket.

*Watch. Wait. Protect.*

She stays.

And listens.

And holds —

Everything she is holding —

With the full force of thirty years of loving people correctly.

And the full knowledge that —

Correctly —

Does not always mean —

Easily.

*End of Chapter Thirty-Six.*

---

## Chapter Thirty-Seven: The Evening Reckoning

---

### I. The Music Continues

The music room.

The afternoon.

The light moving.

The two melodies.

Simultaneously.

The one that resolves.

And the one —

Still —

Asking.

Both real.

Both true.

Both —

Held.

In this room.

By the two women who know —

The full shape of the morning.

And what it cost.

And what it is still —

Quietly —

Costing.

Outside —

London.

The Season.

Moving.

As it always moves.

Regardless.

Toward whatever comes next.

---

### II. Someone Who Is Not Inside It

The music room.

The afternoon.

Both melodies.

Violet — in the chair.

Staying.

As promised.

But —

Underneath the staying —

The thing she cannot set down.

The weight of it.

Increasing.

With every pass of the complicated piece.

Every repetition of the part that has no ending.

She needs —

Someone.

To hold this with her.

Someone who is not —

One of the daughters.

Someone who is not —

Inside it.

The way she is inside it.

One person.

In London.

Who knows enough.

And is trusted enough.

And has the particular quality of intelligence that this situation —

Specifically —

Requires.

Not Lady Danbury.

Not yet.

Lady Danbury will — in her own time — know.

Everything.

She always does.

But Lady Danbury —

Would move.

On it.

Immediately.

With the tactical efficiency of a woman who has been arranging things for fifty years.

And this moment —

Does not yet —

Require arrangement.

It requires —

Understanding.

Daphne.

Who is in this house.

This week.

By her own choice.

Simon's too.

Both of them —

Here.

As though —

They knew.

Something was coming.

That the family would need.

---

### III. Daphne

VIOLET'S PRIVATE SITTING ROOM — FRIDAY EVENING

After the music room.

After Francesca played until the afternoon became evening.

After Violet stayed —

As promised —

Without saying anything.

Which was —

The hardest thing she has done in recent memory.

She finds Daphne.

In the sitting room upstairs.

Simon — out.

At Will Mondrich's.

The Thursday tradition extending to Friday.

Two men.

Occasional cards.

The comfortable company of people who knew each other before titles made things complicated.

Daphne is reading.

Actually reading.

Unlike Eloise — whose reading has been the face-down variety all week —

Daphne reads with the focused attention of a woman who genuinely enjoys books and considers the hour after dinner a gift.

She looks up when Violet enters.

Reads her mother's face.

Sets the book down.

Immediately.

"Mama."

"May I—"

"Sit down," Daphne says.

She does not need to be asked.

She is already moving.

Making room.

The instinctive Daphne accommodation.

Of someone who has been — since birth — the family's first responder.

---

### IV. Not Certain of My Own Judgment

Violet sits.

Beside her.

Looks at her hands.

"I need to tell you something," she says.

"And I need your help."

"With how to — hold it."

"Because—"

She stops.

"Because I am—"

She looks at the window.

"I am not — certain — of my own judgment."

"On this."

"And I need someone—"

"Who is not inside it," Daphne says.

"Yes."

"To help you see it."

"Yes."

Daphne —

Who navigated her own Season's impossible geometry.

Who loved a man who was constitutionally opposed to being loved.

Who fought for it anyway.

Who has been — since Tuesday — watching this family with the clear-eyed attention of someone who came for a brief visit and found something much larger —

Folds her hands.

"Tell me," she says.

---

### V. Everything Told

Violet tells her.

Everything.

The morning room.

Your face.

The hallway.

The music room.

What she heard.

The new piece.

The complicated one.

The conversation with Francesca.

All of it.

Daphne —

Listens.

With the full quality of her attention.

Not managing.

Not arranging.

Simply —

Present.

The clear-eyed Duchess and the eldest daughter.

Both.

Simultaneously.

When Violet finishes —

Daphne is quiet.

For a long moment.

The thinking kind.

"She said no," Daphne says.

"Yes."

"To letting him go."

"Yes."

"Francesca said — directly — that she cannot stop wanting."

"Yes."

"What arrived this morning."

"Yes."

A pause.

"And you believe her."

"I always believe Francesca," Violet says.

"Because she cannot be—"

"False," Daphne says.

"Yes."

---

### VI. Not Immediately

"And the kiss—"

"Was hers," Violet says.

"She chose it."

"Knowing everything."

"Yes."

"Knowing he had chosen Eloise."

"Yes."

"And he—"

Violet stops.

"He didn't—"

Daphne says.

Carefully.

"He didn't step back."

"Immediately."

"No," Violet says.

"Not immediately."

The sitting room.

The evening light.

Both of them.

Sitting with it.

---

### VII. The Specific Fear

"Mama."

"Yes."

"Tell me what you are afraid of."

"Specifically."

"Not—"

"All of it."

"Yes."

"Not the general."

"The specific."

Violet —

Looks at the window.

"I am afraid," she says,

"that Francesca's honesty —"

"Which I love —"

"Which is — entirely — her —"

"Is going to become —"

"A problem."

"Not because she is wrong to feel what she feels."

"But because—"

She stops.

"Because feelings—"

"Don't stay internal," Daphne says.

"No."

"Not when they are — this."

"No."

"They surface."

"In music."

"In expression."

"In — the ten thousand small ways—"

"That people who cannot be false—"

"Reveal themselves," Violet says.

"Yes."

A pause.

"And Eloise—"

"Who notices everything," Daphne says.

"Yes."

"Will notice."

"Yes."

"Eventually."

"Yes."

"And when she does—"

Violet closes her eyes briefly.

"It will not be — the honest kind of hurt," she says.

"It will be—"

"The other kind," Daphne says.

"The ambiguity kind."

"Yes."

"The kind that doesn't end."

"Yes."

---

### VIII. Two True Things

A long silence.

The sitting room.

The evening.

"And him," Daphne says.

"Yes."

"He kissed her back."

"Yes."

"Briefly."

"Yes."

"But—"

"But," Violet says.

"He did."

A pause.

"And he is — currently — in his townhouse."

"Carrying two true things."

"As Francesca is carrying two true things."

"And Eloise—"

"Knows nothing," Daphne says.

"Nothing about the music room."

"Nothing about the kiss."

"Nothing about what Francesca said to you."

"Nothing."

"She is—"

"In the library," Violet says.

"Actually reading."

"Happy."

"The private kind."

"The complete kind."

"The kind she has not—"

"Been," Daphne says.

"In twenty-three years."

"Yes."

---

### IX. The Threads

Daphne stands.

Moves to the window.

Looks at the evening London outside.

The familiar posture.

The Duchess thinking.

The eldest daughter thinking.

Both.

"Simon," she says.

"Yes?"

"He fell into the Arno," she says.

"Checking the book first."

"Yes."

"Which—"

"Means he prioritizes the thing that lasts," Violet says.

"Yes."

"You said."

"Yes."

"And his choice — Eloise — that is the thing that lasts?"

"Yes."

"You believe that."

"I believe—"

Violet pauses.

"I believe he made the right choice," she says.

"For the right reasons."

"And I believe that he knows it."

"Yes."

"Even carrying—"

"What he is carrying."

"Yes."

"From the music room."

"Yes."

A pause.

"The question," Daphne says, "is not whether the choice was right."

"No."

"The question is what happens to all the things that are—"

"Still true," Violet says.

"Despite the choice."

"Yes."

Daphne turns from the window.

"The kiss," she says.

"Yes."

"Was Francesca's first move."

"Yes."

"In—"

She stops.

"In her life," Violet says.

"Practically."

"In that direction."

"Yes."

"With John—"

"He moved first," Violet says.

"Always."

"The gentleness of him."

"Yes."

"And with anyone before—"

"There was no one before."

"No."

"So this—"

"Was the first time," Violet says.

"She reached toward something."

"Of her own choosing."

"Yes."

Daphne is quiet.

"That is not—"

"Nothing," Violet says.

"No."

"It isn't."

---

### X. What Would You Do

"Mama."

"Yes."

"What do you want me to do?"

Violet looks at her eldest daughter.

The Diamond.

The one who set the template.

Who has been — since Tuesday — here.

As though she knew.

"I don't know," Violet says.

"That is why I came to you."

A pause.

"I need you to tell me—"

She stops.

"Am I wrong—"

"To be frightened?"

"No," Daphne says.

Immediately.

"You are not wrong."

"The situation—"

She pauses.

Chooses.

"The situation is — genuinely — complicated."

"Yes."

"In ways that the Season has no mechanism for."

"No."

"Both of them — your daughters — are—"

She stops.

"Feeling something real," she says.

"About the same man."

"Who has made a genuine, honest choice."

"And is — himself — carrying something from this morning."

"Yes."

"That he did not expect to be carrying."

"No."

"And the one who knows the least—"

"Is the one he chose."

"Yes."

"Eloise."

"Who is in the library."

"Actually reading."

"Happy."

"Yes."

---

### XI. It Is My Family

A very long silence.

The sitting room.

The evening.

Both of them.

Inside the full weight of it.

"What would you do?" Violet asks.

"If it were your family."

"If it were—"

She stops.

Daphne looks at her.

"It is my family," she says.

Quietly.

"They are my sisters."

"Yes."

"I know."

---

### XII. The At-Home

She comes back to the settee.

Sits.

Beside her mother.

"I am going to tell you something," she says.

"That I have been—"

"Sitting with."

"Since Tuesday."

"When I came to the house."

"Before any of this."

"Yes."

"When I was simply — observing."

"Yes."

She looks at her hands.

The Duchess's hands.

That have held difficult things before.

"I watched him," she says.

"Mr. Zaff."

"At the at-home."

"Wednesday."

"Yes."

"I watched him — move through the room."

"And I watched — where he went."

"And where he stayed."

A pause.

"He spent twenty minutes with Gregory," she says.

"Telling him about the street people of Florence."

"Not the philosophers."

"The ordinary ones."

"Because Gregory asked."

"Yes."

"And he spent — with Hyacinth — what she described as 'a very productive eight minutes.'"

"Yes."

"And with Benedict—"

"The canvas," Violet says.

"Yes."

"And with me—"

"The morning room."

"Yes."

"And then—"

Daphne pauses.

"He went to the bookshelf."

"And Eloise was there."

"Yes."

"And the distance—"

"Yes," Violet says.

"I saw."

"And then — the window."

"And Francesca."

"Yes."

---

### XIII. Learning the Shape

A pause.

"He moved," Daphne says,

"through that room—"

"As though he was—"

She searches.

"As though he was learning the shape of something," she says.

"Not performing interest."

"Actually learning."

"Every person."

"Every corner."

"Every—"

"Thread," Violet says.

"Yes."

"And by the end of the evening—"

"He had touched every significant thread in the family," Violet says.

"Without appearing to."

"Yes."

A pause.

"That is—"

"Either very deliberate," Daphne says.

"Or completely—"

"Natural," Violet says.

"To him."

"Yes."

"Which is—"

"More alarming," Daphne says.

"Than the deliberate."

"Yes."

They sit with that.

---

### XIV. Present in Every Room

"He is—"

Daphne starts.

"He is going to be in this family's life," she says.

"Whether he chooses Eloise or—"

"He has chosen Eloise."

"Yes."

"Then — as Eloise's—"

She stops.

"Whatever he becomes."

"He is going to be—"

"Present," Violet says.

"Yes."

"In the family."

"In every room."

"At every dinner."

"And Francesca—"

"Will have to—"

"Be present too," Violet says.

"Yes."

"With what she is carrying."

"In every room."

"At every dinner."

"Yes."

---

### XV. It Has to Move

A very long pause.

"Mama."

"Yes."

"I don't think—"

Daphne stops.

Starts again.

"I don't think this is—"

"Something you can manage," she says.

"This one."

"No," Violet says.

"I know."

"It has to—"

"Move at its own pace."

"Yes."

"On its own terms."

"Yes."

"And you—"

"Have to—"

"Watch," Violet says.

"And wait."

"And protect."

"Yes."

"All three."

"Yes."

A pause.

"Simultaneously."

"Yes."

"Which is—"

"The hardest thing," Violet says.

"A mother can do."

Daphne looks at her.

"I know," she says.

"I watched you do it."

"With me."

"And Simon."

"When it was — at its most — impossible."

"You watched," Violet says.

"And waited."

"And protected."

"Yes."

Violet looks at her daughter.

"And I got it wrong," she says.

"Sometimes."

"Yes," Daphne says.

"Sometimes."

"But—"

She takes her mother's hand.

"You were — always — there."

"When it mattered."

"Yes."

"You were always there."

---

### XVI. Not Lady Danbury

The sitting room.

The evening.

The two of them.

Holding the weight between them.

The way the Bridgerton women have always held heavy things.

Together.

Without announcement.

Simply —

Together.

"What about Lady Danbury?" Daphne asks.

"Should I—"

"Not yet," Violet says.

"She will—"

"Move," Daphne says.

"Yes."

"She can't help it."

"No."

"And this—"

"Does not yet require movement."

"No."

"It requires—"

"Stillness," Violet says.

"Yes."

"Which is—"

"Not Lady Danbury's strongest quality," Daphne says.

A pause.

"No," Violet agrees.

"It is not."

---

### XVII. She Cannot Know

"Eloise," Daphne says.

After a moment.

"Yes."

"She cannot know."

"Not yet."

"No."

"Not until—"

"Not until Francesca has—"

"Found her footing," Violet says.

"Yes."

"With it."

"Yes."

"Which will take—"

"Time," Daphne says.

"Yes."

A pause.

"And meanwhile—"

"Eloise is in the library," Violet says.

"Actually reading."

"Happy."

"Yes."

Both of them —

Sitting with the full weight of that.

The specific cruelty of it.

Not cruelty born of malice.

Simply —

The cruelty of timing.

Of two things —

Both true —

Existing simultaneously.

In the same house.

The same family.

The same Friday evening.

---

### XVIII. He Is Carrying This Correctly

"Should I—"

Daphne starts.

"Talk to him?"

"No," Violet says.

"Not yet."

A pause.

"He came to me this morning."

"Before anything."

"As promised."

"Yes."

"And he was honest."

"Yes."

"Even about—"

"His face," Violet says.

"When he came out of the music room."

"Was honest."

"Without him saying a word."

"Yes."

"He is—"

She pauses.

"He is carrying this correctly," she says.

"The best a man can."

"In this situation."

"Yes."

"Which is—"

"Not nothing," Daphne says.

"No."

"But also—"

"Not sufficient," Violet says.

"For what lies ahead."

"No."

A pause.

"Nothing is sufficient," she says.

"For what lies ahead."

"No," Daphne says.

"Nothing is."

---

### XIX. Stay Another Week

The sitting room.

The evening.

London outside.

The Season —

Still moving.

As it always moves.

Regardless.

And through the floors of Bridgerton House —

Faintly —

The music.

From two floors below.

Francesca —

Still playing.

The complicated piece.

The one without an ending.

Both melodies.

Simultaneously.

And down the hall —

The library.

Quiet.

Eloise —

Reading.

Actually.

The particular silence of a woman whose mind has been — finally — released from its own management.

Occupied differently.

With something that does not require management.

Simply —

Presence.

And the letter —

On her desk.

Beside the book.

The answer in English.

Plain.

Still there.

Still real.

Still —

True.

Violet listens to the music.

From two floors above.

Her hands in her lap.

Her daughter beside her.

The note in her pocket.

*Watch. Wait. Protect.*

"Stay," she says.

To Daphne.

Quietly.

"Another week."

"Yes," Daphne says.

Without hesitation.

"Another week."

---

### XX. The Persistence of True Things

The evening deepens.

The music continues.

Both melodies.

Simultaneously.

The one that resolves.

And the one —

Still —

Asking.

With the particular persistence of things that are —

Genuinely —

True.

And will not —

Simply —

Because it would be convenient —

Stop.

*End of Chapter Thirty-Seven.*

---

## Chapter Thirty-Eight: The Reply

---

### I. The Desk

THE ZAFF TOWNHOUSE — FRIDAY EVENING

The reply.

On the desk.

Since Gregory delivered it.

Mid-afternoon.

You were not here when it arrived.

Lucien took it.

Set it on the desk.

Said nothing.

Left it.

Which was — exactly — correct.

The Lucien understanding.

Of things that should not be handed over.

But placed.

Carefully.

And left to be found.

On the terms of the person who needs to find them.

---

### II. The Walking

You came home late.

The Friday evening.

After walking.

Again.

The city absorbing what you needed the city to absorb.

The indifference of London at dusk.

The particular quality of streets that do not know or care what happened in a music room this morning.

The relief of that.

Brief.

But real.

The Thames at twilight.

The bridges.

The ordinary.

Which does not ask anything of you.

Which simply —

Exists.

Around you.

While you carry what you are carrying.

Without requiring you to name it.

Or resolve it.

Or do anything with it at all.

Simply —

Walk.

---

### III. Lucien's Doing

The house quiet when you returned.

Dinner — kept.

Lucien — in the drawing room.

One look at you.

"There's something on your desk," he said.

Not — *a letter arrived.*

Not — *from the Bridgerton house.*

Not — *Gregory brought it.*

Simply —

*There's something on your desk.*

The Lucien precision.

Of a brother who knows —

When to give information.

And when to give space.

"Yes."

"I didn't—"

"I know," you said.

"Thank you."

He nodded.

Let you go.

The particular quality of Lucien letting you go.

Without the follow-up question.

Without the concerned look held a beat too long.

Simply —

The nod.

And the release.

---

### IV. The Study

The study.

The desk.

The lamp — already lit.

Lucien's doing.

Without being asked.

The specific care of a man who does not say *I love you* in those words.

Who says it in lamps.

Left burning.

In rooms you will need.

When you arrive.

The lamp.

The desk.

The physician-architect.

The correspondence volumes.

The philosophy of mathematics.

And —

The reply.

Small.

Sealed.

Her handwriting.

Waiting.

---

### V. Standing

You stand at the desk.

For a moment.

Not sitting.

Simply — standing.

Looking at it.

The weight of the morning still in you.

The music room.

The hands.

The sound she made.

*Home.*

Still there.

Both things.

Both true.

Simultaneously.

The letter on your desk —

From the woman you chose.

The music room in your body —

From the woman who chose you.

Both.

Present.

In the same man.

In the same room.

In the same Friday evening.

You sit.

Pick up the letter.

Hold it.

The specific weight of a reply from Eloise Bridgerton.

Who burned four drafts.

Who asked plainly in English.

Who sent a reply via Gregory on Friday morning.

Having read the answer before nine.

Having read — *yes.*

---

### VI. The Opening

You open it.

The paper unfolds.

One page.

Her handwriting — different from the previous letters.

The previous letters were — constructed.

Even when honest.

The architecture present.

Even when lowered.

The Latin.

The postscripts.

The three of them.

The careful building of a thing she was afraid to build.

This —

Is different.

The handwriting itself —

Looser.

Faster.

As though she wrote it quickly.

Before she could stop herself.

The way you write when you have decided to stop managing.

And mean it.

---

### VII. The Letter

*Mr. Zaff —*

*I have been sitting in the library since your letter arrived.*

*I have read it four times.*

*Not because it was unclear.*

*It was perfectly clear.*

*Clearer than anything anyone has said to me in twenty-three years of people saying things.*

*I have been sitting with it because I find that when something arrives that you have been waiting for without knowing you were waiting —*

*You need to simply —*

*Sit with it.*

*For a while.*

*Before it becomes a thing you have to do something about.*

*I am not good at receiving things.*

*I suspect you know this.*

*I am considerably better at arguing.*

*And finding errors in translations.*

*And sitting on bookshop floors.*

*And burning drafts.*

*And constructing Latin architecture around things I am afraid to say in English.*

*You dismantled all of it.*

*Very quietly.*

*Over three weeks.*

*Without appearing to try.*

*Which is either the most natural thing I have ever witnessed or the most sophisticated approach to a person that has ever been deployed in my direction.*

*I have decided it was natural.*

*Because you are constitutionally honest.*

*And I have — after twenty-three years of being the most perceptive person in most rooms — finally met someone I cannot read strategically.*

*Only — actually.*

*Which is — I find — terrifying.*

*And the only thing I have wanted in twenty-three years.*

*Simultaneously.*

---

### VIII. The Sentence

*You said the direction is mutual.*

*In English.*

*Without architecture.*

*I am going to say something now that I have never said to anyone.*

*Not in Latin.*

*Not in a postscript.*

*Not surrounded by sufficient Aristotle that it can be denied.*

*Simply —*

*I am glad you walked into a coffee house on a Wednesday.*

*I am glad the geography was uncooperative.*

*I am glad — and this is the most alarming sentence I have ever written — that I did not want to leave.*

*Because I have wanted to leave every room I have ever stood in.*

*For twenty-three years.*

*And I did not want to leave that one.*

You stop reading.

Set the letter down.

Not because you are finished.

Because —

That sentence.

Those two lines.

*I have wanted to leave every room I have ever stood in.*

*For twenty-three years.*

*And I did not want to leave that one.*

The weight of them.

What they cost her.

Twenty-three years of standing in rooms with one foot already aimed at the door.

The intellectual escape artist.

The woman who could dismantle any conversation before it reached her.

Who could leave without leaving.

Who could be present and absent simultaneously.

For twenty-three years.

And in a coffee house on Fleet Street —

On a Wednesday —

With the geography uncooperative —

She did not want to leave.

You pick the letter up again.

Continue.

---

### IX. The Rest

*I am telling you this because you asked for honesty.*

*And because the alternative —*

*Surrounding this in Latin until it disappears —*

*Is no longer available to me.*

*You made it unavailable.*

*By being — simply — what you are.*

*Which is the most inconvenient thing anyone has ever done.*

*I would like —*

*Very much —*

*To not be in a bookshop.*

*Or a ball.*

*Or a drawing room.*

*Or surrounded by my family.*

*Who are — collectively — too much of everything.*

*I would like —*

*A conversation.*

*That is simply —*

*Us.*

*Whatever that looks like.*

*I trust you to find it.*

*You found a coffee house.*

*You can find this.*

*— E.*

---

### X. The Postscripts

*P.S. — I told Hyacinth nothing.*

*She knows everything anyway.*

*This is her defining quality and my perpetual burden.*

*P.P.S. — Gregory delivered this letter with the expression of a man who considers himself deeply involved in a situation he was only peripherally asked to assist with.*

*He said — and I quote — "Tell him I said good luck."*

*I am mortified.*

*P.P.P.S. — Three postscripts again.*

*I find I am becoming accustomed to this.*

*Perhaps it is simply how I write now.*

*To you.*

---

### XI. The Reading

The letter.

In your hands.

The lamp.

The desk.

The Friday night.

You read it once.

Slowly.

Every word.

Then —

Again.

The second time slower than the first.

Stopping at —

*I have wanted to leave every room I have ever stood in.*

*For twenty-three years.*

*And I did not want to leave that one.*

Sitting with it.

The weight of it.

What it cost her to write that.

In English.

No Latin.

No architecture.

No postscript to hide it in.

Simply —

There.

On the page.

Given.

The way she gives things when she has decided to give them.

Completely.

Without reservation.

Without the safety net.

The full Eloise.

Arrived.

On one page.

In her own handwriting.

Faster than usual.

Because she meant it too much to slow down.

---

### XII. The Pull

And —

The music room.

Pulling.

From the other direction.

The way it has been pulling since this morning.

The hands.

*Home.*

Both things.

True.

The letter in your right hand —

From the woman who does not want to leave rooms you are in.

The memory in your body —

Of the woman whose hands found the exact shape of something you did not know you were missing.

Both.

Present.

In the same man.

At the same desk.

In the same lamplight.

The honest cost of honest choices.

---

### XIII. Side by Side

You set the letter down.

Carefully.

Beside the other one.

Her letter.

The one you sent.

And its reply.

Side by side.

On the desk.

In the lamplight.

You look at them.

For a long time.

The choice — made.

Still made.

Still right.

Still yours.

And the cost of it —

Sitting beside the rightness.

In the specific way that honest choices always carry honest costs.

Simultaneously.

The rightness does not cancel the cost.

The cost does not diminish the rightness.

Both.

Present.

Fully.

In the lamplight.

On the desk.

Where you sit.

With the pen.

And the pocket watch.

And the full weight of a Friday that began in a music room and is ending here.

---

### XIV. The Pen

You pick up the pen.

Not to write.

Simply — holding it.

The way you hold the pocket watch.

For the weight of it.

The familiar weight of something that does not change.

When everything else is —

In motion.

Kinesis.

Its own state.

Directed.

The terminus —

Present.

Before it is legible.

To the thing in motion.

You are —

In motion.

And the terminus —

Is legible.

Now.

From the inside.

Clearly.

In two directions.

Both real.

Both true.

One chosen.

One —

Carried.

---

### XV. The Invitation

The pen.

The desk.

The two letters.

The lamp.

The Friday night.

London outside.

You write.

Briefly.

A single line.

On a clean piece of paper.

*Sunday.*

*The Serpentine.*

*Ten o'clock.*

*Come alone.*

*— G.*

Not the coffee house.

Not Fleet Street.

Not indoors.

Not a room she would need to leave.

The Serpentine.

Open.

The water.

The sky.

No walls.

No rooms.

No doors.

Simply —

The park.

And the two of you.

Walking.

Which is what you do.

When you need to think.

And what she does.

When she needs to breathe.

Both.

Simultaneously.

She said — *I trust you to find it.*

You found it.

---

### XVI. The Dark

You seal it.

Set it aside for morning.

Blow out the lamp.

Sit in the dark for a moment.

Both letters beside you.

The music room in you.

The reply in you.

*Home* in you.

*Not alone* in you.

Both.

Simultaneously.

Carried.

The dark study.

The desk.

The pocket watch.

The pen.

The physician-architect.

The man who checks the book first.

And falls into the Arno regardless.

You go to bed.

Do not sleep immediately.

But eventually.

The particular sleep —

Of a man who is —

Honest.

About everything.

Including the things that cost.

*End of Chapter Thirty-Eight.*

---

## Chapter Thirty-Nine: The Music Room

---

### I. The Library

BRIDGERTON HOUSE — FRIDAY EVENING

The library.

Eloise.

The book — actually being read.

For the third hour.

Which is — for Eloise — not unusual.

She has always read for three hours at a stretch.

But the quality of it today —

Different.

The words landing.

Staying.

The mind — present.

Not managing.

Not composing Latin arguments.

Not burning drafts.

Simply —

Here.

Reading.

The specific luxury of a mind released from its own management.

---

### II. The Desk

She finishes a chapter.

Sets the book down.

Stretches.

The Friday evening around her.

The house — its sounds.

Dinner — somewhere below.

The family — assembling.

The ordinary Friday.

She looks at the desk.

The letter.

His.

Still there.

Beside the book she is reading.

She put it there deliberately.

Not to re-read.

Simply —

To have it near.

Which is —

New.

For Eloise.

Who has never kept letters near.

Who has kept everything at arm's length.

As a matter of principle.

And survival.

She looks at it.

The handwriting.

The English.

The *yes.*

Plain.

Without architecture.

Given.

---

### III. The Trust

She thinks about Sunday.

The Serpentine.

His reply — not yet received.

But —

She trusts him to find it.

She said so.

In the letter.

And she meant it.

Which is —

In itself —

Something.

She does not —

As a rule —

Trust people to find things.

She finds them herself.

Because trusting requires —

Vulnerability.

The specific vulnerability of relinquishing control.

Which she has spent twenty-three years perfecting the avoidance of.

The armour.

The Latin.

The leaving rooms before they can matter.

And now —

She has given the armour away.

In a letter.

Written fast.

In English.

And she is —

Sitting here.

Without it.

In the library.

On a Friday evening.

And —

It is —

Fine.

More than fine.

It is —

The thing she did not know she wanted.

The absence of the weight she did not know she was carrying.

The specific relief of having said the true thing.

And survived.

---

### IV. The Arrival

She stops.

The thought.

Mid-sentence.

Something —

Arrives.

From outside the library.

Through the walls.

Faint.

But —

Present.

The music.

From two floors below.

Francesca.

Still.

Playing.

---

### V. Listening

Eloise —

Goes still.

In the chair.

Listening.

The piece —

She has heard it since this morning.

All day.

In fragments.

Through the walls.

Through the floors.

The complicated one.

The new one.

The one that arrived today.

That she has been —

If she is honest —

Not entirely listening to.

Because she has been —

Occupied.

Differently.

With her own interior.

Her own arrived thing.

Her own —

Window unlocked.

Air getting in.

But now —

She listens.

Properly.

The way she listens to everything.

With the full force of her attention.

The quality that makes her —

The most extraordinary person in most rooms.

Applied —

For the first time today —

To something outside herself.

---

### VI. The Frown

The piece —

Is —

She frowns.

Slightly.

The way she frowns when something requires more examination.

When a text does not yield its meaning on the first reading.

When the Latin contains a subordinate clause she did not expect.

The piece is —

Not the resolved melody.

Not the searching one.

Not any of the pieces she knows.

Of the considerable Francesca repertoire she has catalogued across twenty-three years of sharing walls with a sister who speaks in music.

This is —

New.

Arrived today.

Both melodies.

Simultaneously.

One —

She recognises.

The resolved one.

The one from the musicale.

More certain than before.

More —

Itself.

And the other —

New.

Different.

Complicated.

In the structural sense.

The way music is complicated when —

She stops.

The thought.

Again.

---

### VII. Francesca's Direction

Looks at the wall.

Between the library and the rest of the house.

Francesca's direction.

Two floors below.

The music room.

Where Francesca has been —

All day.

Friday morning.

Friday afternoon.

Friday evening.

All day.

The full Friday.

Without coming out.

Without breakfast.

Without lunch.

Without the morning room.

Without the family.

All day.

In the music room.

With a new piece.

That arrived this morning.

---

### VIII. The Full Force

Eloise thinks.

With the full force of her particular intelligence.

Applied — for the first time today — in a direction that is not —

Herself.

Or Giordhyn.

Or Latin.

Or the direction of the terminus.

Applied —

To Francesca.

Her sister.

Whom she loves —

Completely.

In the specific Eloise way.

Which is —

Not demonstrative.

Not loud.

Not orchestrated.

But —

Absolute.

The way Eloise does everything she actually means.

Absolutely.

Without qualification.

Without half-measures.

The full force.

Applied now.

To the question she has not been asking.

All day.

Because she was too happy to ask it.

---

### IX. When

When did she last —

She thinks.

When did she last actually check on Francesca.

Today.

Friday.

Today.

She —

Received a letter.

And went to the morning room.

And Mama was there.

And the family assembled.

And Colin said nothing — which was generous —

And Gregory said *I'm glad* — through the door — which was genuine —

And Hyacinth noted everything — which was inevitable —

And Eloise went back to the library.

And read.

For three hours.

And was —

Happy.

The private kind.

The complete kind.

And Francesca —

Was not in the morning room.

Was not part of the assembly.

Was not —

Present.

In any of it.

---

### X. The Occupation

Eloise —

Who notices everything —

Did not notice.

This.

Because she was —

Occupied.

With her own interior.

For the first time in twenty-three years —

With something other than —

Management and refusal and the architecture of not-wanting.

She was occupied with —

Wanting.

Simply.

And the wanting —

Took up the entire available space.

Left no room for —

Outward observation.

Even of the people she loves.

Even of Francesca.

Who was —

All day —

Two floors below.

In the music room.

Playing a piece that did not exist yesterday.

And Eloise —

Did not notice.

The guilt of that —

Arriving now.

Quietly.

The specific guilt of a woman who prides herself on noticing everything.

And missed —

The most important thing.

In her own house.

On the most important day.

---

### XI. The Music Continues

The music continues.

Two floors below.

The complicated piece.

Both melodies.

The one that resolves.

And the other.

The new one.

That has no ending.

Eloise frowns.

More deeply.

The particular frown of a woman who is —

Beginning —

To read something.

That she does not yet have sufficient information to fully interpret.

But whose shape —

Is —

Beginning —

To suggest —

Something.

---

### XII. The Assembling

She thinks about this morning.

What she knows.

The letter arrived.

She read it.

She went to the morning room.

Mama was there.

The family.

The happiness.

And —

Giordhyn —

She thinks.

He sent the letter before nine.

Which means he was already —

Out.

Early.

Before the letter arrived.

He was somewhere.

Before nine.

On a Friday morning.

Before he sent the letter.

Or —

At the same time.

Or —

She stops.

A thought arriving.

Quietly.

From the direction of the thing she is not yet fully seeing.

He came here.

Didn't he.

This morning.

Before the letter arrived.

He would have —

He is the kind of man who —

She knows this about him.

From three weeks of knowing him properly.

He prioritises the thing that lasts.

He checks the book first.

He comes to the mother before the Season.

He tells the truth —

Before someone else does.

That is —

Who he is.

Constitutionally.

He came here.

This morning.

Before the letter.

Which means —

He told someone —

Before Eloise read the *yes.*

Someone in this house.

Knew —

Before Eloise.

---

### XIII. Who

Who.

The music.

Two floors below.

Both melodies.

Simultaneously.

Eloise —

Very still.

In the library chair.

The book in her lap.

Closed now.

Without her noticing it close.

The letter on the desk.

His handwriting.

The *yes.*

She is not —

She tells herself carefully —

She is not making assumptions.

She is —

Observing.

Which is her defining quality.

And her perpetual —

Occupation.

---

### XIV. The Evidence

Mama.

Who received her this morning.

With the warmth.

And the iron.

Both.

And said —

*Tell me.*

And listened.

Without pushing.

Without managing.

The practicing —

Paying off.

As Eloise noted.

At the time.

But Mama was —

Already —

In the morning room.

When Eloise arrived.

Not reading.

Not writing.

Simply —

There.

As though she had been —

Waiting.

For something to arrive.

Which —

At the time —

Eloise attributed to maternal instinct.

Mothers always know.

Francesca said this once.

*She always knows.*

---

### XV. The New Piece

Francesca.

The music.

Two floors below.

The complicated piece.

New.

Arrived today.

All day.

Since —

This morning.

Eloise sets the book on the desk.

Stands.

Goes to the door.

Opens it.

The hallway.

The Bridgerton house Friday evening.

The sounds of the family somewhere below.

Dinner —

Probably concluded.

The family dispersing.

The ordinary Friday.

---

### XVI. The Descent

She goes to the stairs.

Descends.

One floor.

Two.

The music louder now.

Through the closed door.

Of the music room.

She stops.

Outside it.

Listening.

Properly.

For the first time today.

The piece —

Up close.

Different from through walls.

More —

Detailed.

More —

Present.

The two melodies.

She hears them both.

Separately.

The way you hear individual instruments in an orchestra when you know what to listen for.

The resolved one — present.

More certain than the musicale.

Good.

Right.

Francesca finding her way.

As she has been finding it.

Since —

Before the musicale.

Before —

Any of this.

Her own recovery.

Her own terms.

And the other.

The new one.

Running alongside.

Complicated.

Not resolving.

The way —

She has heard Francesca play when —

Processing something.

That has not yet —

Found its shape.

Or its ending.

---

### XVII. The Hand

Eloise —

Stands at the door.

Hand raised.

To knock.

Or not.

She thinks about Francesca.

Since John.

The eight months.

The beside-the-pianoforte.

The not-playing.

The Scotland.

The return.

The sitting beside the thing she loved without being able to reach it.

And then —

The musicale.

The resolution.

The resolved melody.

More certain every day.

She thinks about the Danbury ball.

Three weeks ago.

A man in midnight blue.

A pianoforte corner.

Lady Danbury's introduction.

Francesca's smile.

Italicized.

She thinks about the at-home.

Wednesday.

The drawing room window.

Francesca and Giordhyn.

Together.

Briefly.

The garden beyond the glass.

The roses.

What Eloise saw.

From across the room.

Which is —

What sent her to the bookshelves.

What produced the managed distance.

The thin kind.

That did not manage.

She thinks about the musicale.

The resolution.

Thursday.

And what she noticed.

Across the room.

His face.

When Francesca played.

The quality of his attention.

Which she —

At the time —

Filed.

Under —

*Complicated.*

---

### XVIII. The Gap in the Text

And now —

Friday.

The new piece.

All day.

Arrived this morning.

Both melodies.

Simultaneously.

Eloise —

At the door.

Her hand —

Still raised.

Not yet knocked.

The intelligence that she was born with —

The particular quality that makes her —

The most extraordinary person in most rooms —

Assembling.

Rapidly.

The way it always assembles.

Even when —

She would prefer —

It didn't.

She does not yet have —

The full picture.

She is missing —

Something.

Several somethings.

She knows this.

The instinct of a reader who can feel the gap in the text.

Even without knowing what fills it.

But she has —

Enough.

To know that Francesca's Friday —

Was —

Different.

From Eloise's Friday.

In ways she cannot yet —

Fully name.

But whose shape —

Is —

Beginning to be legible.

---

### XIX. The Knock

The terminus —

She thinks.

Involuntarily.

*Present before it is legible.*

*To the thing in motion.*

Even when the thing in motion —

Does not yet know —

It is moving.

She knocks.

Softly.

"Frannie."

The childhood name.

The one that doesn't come out in company.

The one that belongs to the two of them.

Before everything.

Before John.

Before the Season.

Before any of it.

Simply —

The two of them.

Sisters.

In the margins.

Together.

The music —

Stops.

The particular stopping of someone who was playing in the dark and did not expect the door.

A brief silence.

Then —

"Come in."

---

### XX. The Composure

THE MUSIC ROOM — FRIDAY EVENING

Francesca —

At the pianoforte.

Hands in her lap.

The music stopped.

The expression —

The composure.

Present.

The iron Francesca variety.

That has been — all day — thinner than usual.

And is — at this specific moment —

Working harder than it should have to.

At the specific task of appearing —

Normal.

Simply —

Francesca.

At the pianoforte.

On a Friday evening.

As she always is.

Nothing different.

Nothing arrived.

Nothing carried.

---

### XXI. The Shift

She sees Eloise.

In the doorway.

The expression —

Shifting.

Fractionally.

Something moving beneath the composure.

Too quickly for most people to catch.

Not Eloise.

Eloise catches everything.

Even when —

She doesn't yet know —

What she is catching.

"Eloise," she says.

"Frannie."

---

### XXII. Side by Side

Eloise crosses the room.

Sits.

Not the chair by the window.

The bench.

Beside her.

The way she sat beside her in Scotland.

The way they have — always — been.

When it matters.

Side by side.

Without requiring the other to be anything other than what they are.

A silence.

The music room.

The evening.

The last light through the window.

The garden.

The roses.

Still not yet.

---

### XXIII. How Are You

"You've been in here all day," Eloise says.

"Yes."

"The whole day."

"Yes."

"You weren't at breakfast."

"No."

"Or lunch."

"No."

A pause.

"Are you—"

She stops.

Starts again.

"How are you?"

Not —

The social version.

Not —

The managed version.

The Eloise version.

Which is —

Direct.

Undecorated.

Actually wanting to know.

---

### XXIV. I'm All Right

Francesca —

Looks at her hands.

The keys.

The sheet music.

The complicated piece.

Unfinished.

No ending yet.

"I'm—"

She starts.

The composure —

Working.

Hard.

"I'm all right."

Eloise —

Who notices everything —

Hears this.

The specific quality of *I'm all right* from Francesca.

Which has always meant —

Not all right.

In a way she will not specify.

Because Francesca does not specify.

Unless —

She decides to.

On her own terms.

In her own time.

"Good," Eloise says.

Simply.

Not pushing.

Not the Eloise default.

The practicing.

Paying off.

In a different direction from Violet.

But —

The same principle.

Learned.

From watching her mother practice it.

This week.

In this house.

---

### XXV. The Letter

She sits beside Francesca.

In the quiet.

The bench.

The music room.

The evening.

"I received a letter today," Eloise says.

Quietly.

"I know," Francesca says.

Eloise looks at her.

"Mama told you."

"Yes."

"And—"

A pause.

"And the family assembled," Francesca says.

"In the morning room."

"Yes."

"Without me."

"Yes."

---

### XXVI. Don't Apologize

A pause.

"I'm sorry," Eloise says.

"I should have—"

"No," Francesca says.

"Don't."

"I should have come to find you—"

"Eloise."

"I was — in the library — all day — and I didn't—"

"Eloise."

Francesca turns.

Looks at her sister.

The full look.

The depth.

The ocean.

Fully present.

"Don't apologize," she says.

"For being happy."

"That is not—"

"Something to apologize for."

The music room.

The two of them.

Side by side.

The bench.

The evening light.

---

### XXVII. The Reading

Eloise —

Looks at Francesca.

Reading.

As she always reads.

People.

The way she reads texts.

For what is actually there.

Not what is performed.

Francesca —

Meeting her gaze.

The composure —

Present.

The iron —

Present.

Both.

Working.

Hard.

At the specific task of appearing —

Glad.

For her sister.

Which she is.

Genuinely.

And —

Simultaneously —

Other things.

Which she is also.

Genuinely.

Both true.

Simultaneously.

Eloise —

Reads this.

Reads the both.

The simultaneously.

Without —

Yet —

Having the words for what she is reading.

The gap in the text.

Present.

Palpable.

Something —

Not said.

Something —

Carried.

In the composure.

Behind the iron.

In the new piece.

Without an ending.

She does not ask.

Not yet.

Because —

She does not yet have —

Sufficient information.

To ask the right question.

And Eloise —

Who argues with precision —

Does not ask questions she cannot yet frame correctly.

---

### XXVIII. Play Something

"Play something," she says instead.

Quietly.

"Anything."

"Not the—"

She gestures.

Vaguely.

At the sheet music.

The complicated piece.

"Something else."

"Something you know."

Francesca —

Looks at her sister.

The gratitude —

Briefly —

Visible.

The specific gratitude of someone being given permission to —

Simply —

Be.

Without explaining.

Without performing.

With someone who loves them.

And is asking —

Not for clarity —

But for company.

---

### XXIX. The Old Piece

She turns.

To the keys.

Plays.

Something old.

Something she has known since childhood.

Simple.

Complete.

The melody that has always been —

Simply —

What it is.

Without complication.

Without countermelody.

Without the new piece's impossible complexity.

Simply —

Music.

Known.

Familiar.

Safe.

Eloise —

Sits beside her.

On the bench.

Listening.

Actually.

The way she listened in the bookshop.

For the intention.

Not the performance.

From beside.

Not from across.

Not from the observer's position.

From —

Within the moment.

As she is —

Learning —

Slowly —

To be.

---

### XXX. Both Carrying

The music room.

The evening.

The two sisters.

Side by side.

The bench.

One playing.

One listening.

Both —

Present.

Both —

Carrying something.

The other does not yet —

Fully —

Know.

Eloise —

Carrying the letter.

The *yes.*

The window unlocked.

The air.

The happiness.

The private kind.

The complete kind.

And —

Underneath it —

The beginning of a question.

She cannot yet frame.

About the piece that arrived today.

About Francesca's Friday.

About the morning.

And what she does not know.

But whose shape —

Is —

Beginning —

To be legible.

Francesca —

Carrying everything she told Violet.

The flame.

The morning.

The kiss.

The cannot-stop-wanting.

The constitutionally-incapable-of-false.

And —

Underneath it —

Her sister.

Beside her.

On the bench.

Innocent.

Completely.

Happily.

Entirely.

Unaware.

And the guilt of that —

Sitting —

In the same place —

As the flame.

Simultaneously.

Both real.

Both true.

Both —

Hers.

To carry.

For now.

In the only language she has.

The music.

The old simple piece.

Playing.

For her sister.

Who loves her.

And is sitting beside her.

And does not yet know.

What the morning held.

And what it left behind.

---

### XXXI. The House

The music room.

The Friday evening.

The two of them.

Side by side.

The bench.

The music.

Simple.

Old.

Known.

And through the walls —

Faintly —

The house around them.

Violet — upstairs.

Carrying everything.

With the care it deserves.

And Daphne — beside her.

Staying another week.

And across the park —

The Zaff townhouse.

The desk.

The lamp.

Two letters.

Side by side.

In the lamplight.

And a single line —

Already written.

*Sunday.*

*The Serpentine.*

*Ten o'clock.*

*Come alone.*

The Season —

Still moving.

As it always moves.

Regardless.

Toward whatever comes next.

Which is —

As always —

Both more and less complicated.

Than anyone anticipated.

When it began.

*End of Chapter Thirty-Nine.*

---

## Chapter Forty: The Weekend

---

### I. The Weight of Friday

*The weekend.*

*Saturday and Sunday.*

*Both days carrying the weight of Friday.*

*In different rooms.*

*In different ways.*

*By different people.*

*All of whom are —*

*Feeling something —*

*They cannot entirely name.*

---

### II. The Ordinary Saturday

SATURDAY — BRIDGERTON HOUSE

The family assembles for breakfast.

As it always does.

The ordinary Saturday.

Except —

Not.

---

### III. The Table

The table.

Violet at one end.

Daphne beside her.

Colin and Penelope.

Gregory — the appointed seat.

Hyacinth — already talking.

Anthony and Kate — arrived late.

The full assembly.

Or —

Almost.

---

### IV. Two Chairs

Two chairs.

Adjacent.

Both occupied.

Both —

Present.

In the physical sense.

Completely.

Eloise.

Francesca.

Side by side.

As they sat on the bench Friday evening.

The music room.

The old simple piece.

That evening —

Was —

Fine.

They were fine.

The company without explanation.

The sitting beside.

Both of them.

Each carrying her own thing.

And neither —

Requiring the other —

To know it.

---

### V. The Shift

But this morning —

The table —

Is different.

Not dramatically.

Not the Bridgerton variety of different —

Which announces itself.

Loudly.

With momentum.

And volume.

And the accumulated energy of eight children who have never once in their collective lives underreacted to anything.

Simply —

Different.

In the quiet way.

The way a room is different when a window has been opened.

And closed again.

And the air —

Has changed.

Slightly.

But permanently.

And no one can quite locate the source of the change.

Simply —

Feel it.

---

### VI. The Bread

Eloise —

Passes the bread to Francesca.

Without looking.

The habitual gesture.

Twenty-three years of breakfast tables.

The automatic accommodation.

Bread passed.

Before asked.

Francesca —

Takes it.

Without looking.

The habitual receiving.

Twenty-three years of breakfast tables.

The automatic receipt.

Bread taken.

Before thanked.

But —

In the passing.

In the receiving.

The briefest —

Fractional —

Not-quite-meeting.

Of their eyes.

The almost-glance.

That veers.

At the last possible moment.

Away.

---

### VII. The Three Who Notice

Nobody notices.

Except —

Violet.

Who notices everything.

And Daphne.

Whose eyes are — currently — everywhere.

And Penelope.

Whose particular quality of attention —

Misses nothing.

Ever.

In any room.

The three of them —

Do not look at each other.

Across the table.

They don't need to.

They are —

Separately —

Filing the same thing.

In the same cabinet.

Marked —

*Watch. Wait. Protect.*

---

### VIII. The Machinery

The breakfast continues.

Hyacinth talks.

Gregory contributes.

Colin makes someone laugh.

Anthony has opinions about something.

Kate redirects them.

The ordinary magnificent Bridgerton breakfast machinery.

Operating.

At full capacity.

---

### IX. Eloise's Quiet

And Eloise —

Who is usually —

The most vocal presence at any table she occupies —

Is —

Quiet.

Not the face-down-book quiet.

Not the managing-distance quiet.

A different quiet.

The quiet of a woman who is —

Inside something.

And trying to understand its shape.

From within.

Which is —

Not her natural direction.

She usually understands things from outside.

The observer.

The cataloguer.

The reader of rooms.

Not the inhabitant of them.

---

### X. Francesca's Quiet

And Francesca —

Who is always —

Quiet —

Is quiet in a way that is —

Different from her usual quiet.

Her usual quiet is —

Complete.

Self-contained.

The quiet of a woman who has everything she needs inside herself.

And does not require the room to supplement it.

This quiet is —

The other kind.

The carrying kind.

The weight-of-something kind.

That looks identical from the outside.

But feels —

From the inside —

Entirely different.

---

### XI. Anthony

Anthony —

Who has been —

The Viscount —

For long enough —
